Background¶

In this assignment, convolutional neural networks (CNNs) are built to classify whether images contain a dog (1) or cat(0). To facilitate building the neural networks, a 2x2 design is considered to test the impact of different hyperparamters. The training set contains 25,000 images, while the test set contains 12,500 images. The test set predictions have a goal of being submitted to Kaggle for scoring. The tflearn package is used to generate models due to its brevity. Commentary is provided at the end of the assignment on the best model to use for image classification.

Data Initation and Preparation¶

In this section, we read in the data and prepare it for modeling. Along the way, checks are performed to ensure that the data is read in properly.

In [1]:
RANDOM_SEED = 42

Based on the naming convetion shown above, we can see that we can easily code each picture that has 'cat' in the name as a 0, and each picture that has 'dog' in the name as a 1. Additionally, in reading in the data, we resize each image to a more managemeable size (64 x 64) and standardize the data by dividing by 255. We can normalize the data this way to get all values scaled between 0-1. Therefore, to read in all pictures, we need to loop through each one to perform these computations.

In [4]:
main_dir = "/Users/juliaellis/Documents/NU/422 - Machine Learning/R Code"
train_dir = "dogs-vs-cats/train"
train_path = os.path.join(main_dir,train_dir)

#https://www.kaggle.com/shrawansapre/dogs-vs-cats-convolutional-neural-nets
label=[]
data=[]

#Loop iterating over each file in the training folder
for file in tqdm(os.listdir(train_path)):
    #Reading every image and converting it to grayscale
    image=cv2.imread(os.path.join(train_path,file), cv2.IMREAD_GRAYSCALE)
    #Resizing the image into a manageable size
    image=cv2.resize(image,(64,64))
    #If a file name starts with "cat"
    if file.startswith("cat"):
        label.append(0)
    elif file.startswith("dog"):
        label.append(1)
    try:
        data.append(image/255) 
    except:
        label=label[:len(label)-1]

Before we dive into modeling, there are a few additional checks that shoiuld be performed.

In [11]:

From the output above, we can see that the first entry has a min value just above 0 and a max value just below 1. Therefore, our data standardization has been performed successfully. Next, we examine the distribution of cats and dogs.

In [26]:
num_zeros = (y_train == 0).sum()
num_ones = (y_train == 1).sum()
print("The number of cats is: ", num_zeros)
print("The number of dogs is: ", num_ones)

The number of cats is:  12500
The number of dogs is:  12500

We can see that there is a 50/50 split of cat and dog images, which is a good sign. Otherwise, work would have had to be done to create a more even class distribution, which would help ensure we generate an unbiased model.

Given we have read in all 25,000 train and 12,500 test images and verified that data standardization and the class distribution are sufficient for modeling, we are ready to move on to the model building phase.

Model Building¶

In this assignment, a 2x2 factorial design is considered to test the performance of different hyperparameters within a CNN model.

For context/background, the overall architecture of a CNN consists of an input layer, hidden layer(s), and an output layer. One of the main building blocks of a CNN is the convolutional layer, which is a type of hidden layer whose main goal is to a receive a feature map. Convolutional layers help with the detection of low-level features within an image.

Because CNN models are complex and require a decent number of layers to have 'good' performance, some additional explanation is needed before moving forward.

First, in this assignment, all pooling layers used will be max pooling layers. This decision was made because "a max pooling layer is the most common type of pooling layer" (Geron, p. 372).

Second, after each convolutional layer, a max pooling layer is used. This decision was made to reduce dimensionality and help protect against overfitting.

Third, another overfitting technique that is used is a dropout layer, which drops unuseful neurons.

Fourth, the adam optimizer is used when fitting all models. This optimizer is a popular optimizer, and because it is not gradient descent, then processing times are reduced.

Below is a table that displays the experimental factors used in this assignment. The number of layers that are changed is removing a convolutional and max pooling layer (which are considered one layer).

In [104]:
a = PrettyTable()
a.field_names = ["Model Name","Number of Layers","Learning Rate"]
a.add_row(["Model One",6,.0001])
a.add_row(["Model Two",6,.01])
a.add_row(["Model Three",7,.0001])
a.add_row(["Model Four",7,.01])
print(a.get_string(title="CNN Experimental Factors"))

Baseline Model¶

Before generating the four models above, a baseline model is generated to gauge initial test set performance and code. Of note, a validation set is not used here, but based on performance, this can be used to inform the model approach for the abovementioned models. The baseline model is also used to help validate that the above factors are good factors for testing.

In [38]:
#https://mc.ai/cats-and-dogs-classifier-convolutional-neural-network-with-python-and-tensorflow-9-steps-of/
import tflearn
from tflearn.layers.conv import conv_2d, max_pool_2d
from tflearn.layers.core import input_data, dropout, fully_connected
from tflearn.layers.estimator import regression

MODEL_NAME = 'dogs-vs-cats-convnet-m2'
IMAGE_SIZE = 64
tf.reset_default_graph()

convnet = input_data(shape=[None, IMAGE_SIZE, IMAGE_SIZE, 1], name='input')

#Conv Layer 1
convnet = conv_2d(convnet, 32, 5, activation='relu')
convnet = max_pool_2d(convnet, 5)

#Conv Layer 2
convnet = conv_2d(convnet, 64, 5, activation='relu')
convnet = max_pool_2d(convnet, 5)

#Conv Layer 3
convnet = conv_2d(convnet, 128, 5, activation='relu')
convnet = max_pool_2d(convnet, 5)

#Conv Layer 4 - could also pull this
convnet = conv_2d(convnet, 64, 5, activation='relu')
convnet = max_pool_2d(convnet, 5)

#Conv Layer 5
convnet = conv_2d(convnet, 32, 5, activation='relu')
convnet = max_pool_2d(convnet, 5)

#Conv Layer 6
convnet = fully_connected(convnet, 1024, activation='relu')
convnet = dropout(convnet, 0.5)

#Fully Connected Layer with SoftMax as Activation Function
convnet = fully_connected(convnet, 1, activation='softmax') 
#this is supposed to be a 2 here b/c we have 2 outputs but this throws an error below

#Regression for ConvNet with ADAM optimizer
convnet = regression(convnet, optimizer='adam', learning_rate=.0001, loss='categorical_crossentropy', name='targets')
convnet=tflearn.reshape(convnet,[-1]) #saw this online so adding this to see if it helps

model = tflearn.DNN(convnet, tensorboard_dir='log')

Remaining Models¶

Due to time constraints, this code has not been able to be executed. However, it is provided here to show directionally the next steps for the assignment.

To make it easier to generate the four models listed above, two functions are defined to aid in model building. The first function contains all layers, while the second function is the same as the full function except layer four has been removed.

In [ ]:
# define functions to build models
def cnn_model_full(IMAGE_SIZE, MODEL_NAME, LEARNING_RATE):
    tf.reset_default_graph()
    convnet = input_data(shape=[None, IMAGE_SIZE, IMAGE_SIZE, 1], name='input')

    #Conv Layer 1
    convnet = conv_2d(convnet, 32, 5, activation='relu')
    convnet = max_pool_2d(convnet, 5)

    #Conv Layer 2
    convnet = conv_2d(convnet, 64, 5, activation='relu')
    convnet = max_pool_2d(convnet, 5)

    #Conv Layer 3
    convnet = conv_2d(convnet, 128, 5, activation='relu')
    convnet = max_pool_2d(convnet, 5)

    #Conv Layer 4
    convnet = conv_2d(convnet, 64, 5, activation='relu')
    convnet = max_pool_2d(convnet, 5)

    #Conv Layer 5
    convnet = conv_2d(convnet, 32, 5, activation='relu')
    convnet = max_pool_2d(convnet, 5)

    #Conv Layer 6
    convnet = fully_connected(convnet, 1024, activation='relu')
    convnet = dropout(convnet, 0.5)

    #Fully Connected Layer with SoftMax as Activation Function
    convnet = fully_connected(convnet, 2, activation='softmax')

    #Regression for ConvNet with ADAM optimizer
    convnet = regression(convnet, optimizer='adam', learning_rate=LEARNING_RATE, loss='categorical_crossentropy', name='targets')

    model = tflearn.DNN(convnet, tensorboard_dir='log')

    # Train model for 3 epochs
    model.fit(X_train, y_train, n_epoch=3,
        snapshot_step=1000, show_metric=True, run_id=MODEL_NAME, val_feed_dicts=0.20) #20% of data used for validation
    
    model.save(MODEL_NAME)

# removed layer 4
def cnn_model_sub(IMAGE_SIZE, MODEL_NAME, LEARNING_RATE):
    tf.reset_default_graph()
    convnet = input_data(shape=[None, IMAGE_SIZE, IMAGE_SIZE, 1], name='input')

    #Conv Layer 1
    convnet = conv_2d(convnet, 32, 5, activation='relu')
    convnet = max_pool_2d(convnet, 5)

    #Conv Layer 2
    convnet = conv_2d(convnet, 64, 5, activation='relu')
    convnet = max_pool_2d(convnet, 5)

    #Conv Layer 3
    convnet = conv_2d(convnet, 128, 5, activation='relu')
    convnet = max_pool_2d(convnet, 5)

    #Conv Layer 5
    convnet = conv_2d(convnet, 32, 5, activation='relu')
    convnet = max_pool_2d(convnet, 5)

    #Conv Layer 6
    convnet = fully_connected(convnet, 1024, activation='relu')
    convnet = dropout(convnet, 0.5)

    #Fully Connected Layer with SoftMax as Activation Function
    convnet = fully_connected(convnet, 2, activation='softmax')

    #Regression for ConvNet with ADAM optimizer
    convnet = regression(convnet, optimizer='adam', learning_rate=LEARNING_RATE, loss='categorical_crossentropy', name='targets')

    model = tflearn.DNN(convnet, tensorboard_dir='log')

    # Train model for 3 epochs
    model.fit(X_train, y_train, n_epoch=3,
        snapshot_step=1000, show_metric=True, run_id=MODEL_NAME, val_feed_dicts=0.20) #20% of data used for validation
    
    model.save(MODEL_NAME)

Summary and Conclusions¶

In this assignment, CNNs were considered to identify/classify dog and cat images. Although this assignment was not able to be fully completed due to extensive time needed to get tensorflow and tflearn installed, there are still some learnings that can be shared.

Regular neural networks do not scale well for image classification. For instance, with larger image sizes, the number of weights used in each hidden layer significantly increases. By having so many neurons, this kind of model not only has greater potential to overfit, but it also incurs large processing times by using a fully connected structure. Thus, CNNs take the structure of regular neural nets, but instead of connecting all neurons together within each hidden layer, each layer is connected to a small region of the layer before it. By building a model using a sequence of layers, processing times are reduced (i.e., output converges) and there is greater flexibility to protect against overfitting.

In short, CNNs are the best structure for image classification because they take advantage of the image structure and transform image layers into an output format. They have the best chance for achieving higher model accuracy. However, CNNs are an extremely complex deep learning model, and as such, ample knowledge is needed to implement them properly.

Due to time constraints, there were several parts of this assignment that were cut short that should be explored to ensure the optimal model is developed. Some examples are:

  • Consider implementing and comparing performance using common CNN architectures. These architectures were consulted to get a general framework for CNN structure (i.e., number and type of layers) but were not used for comparative purposes.
  • More time should be spent on tuning hyperparameters and the type of layers. As one example, max pooling layers were only considered. Performance could be compared using average pooling layers. Also, more learning rates, different drop out rates, zero padding, and increasing the number of epochs could/should be used to determine their performance impact. These techniques help to protect against overfitting and provide context on what parameters work best for this problem.
  • While only CNN was considered for this assignment, an MLP classifier could be used as a point of comparison to help provide further evidence of the power/benefit of a CNN.
  • The level of business understanding should be better understood and used to inform the model/architecture for the model. If accuracy is most important, then a compromise is needed, as the end model is more of a 'black box' process.

When working with CNNs or deep learning models, it should be fully noted that ample time is needed to ensure that system requirements are met for working with the appropriate packages. Memory issues may also be encountered so this should be taken into consideration with more time added to allow for unexpected behavior.
